.cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-ohg-ai-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ohg-ai_76-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Before /r/, /h/ (including when derived from Proto-Germanic /x\xca\xb7/) or /w/, or word-finally.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-ohg-au-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ohg-au_77-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ohg-au_77-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Before /h/ (including when derived from Proto-Germanic /x\xca\xb7/) or before any <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental consonant</a>, i.e. /s/,/z/,/\xc3\xbe/,/t/,/d/,/r/,/l/,/n/.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-dental-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-dental_78-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Before any <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental consonant</a>, i.e. /s/,/z/,/\xc3\xbe/,/t/,/d/,/r/,/l/,/n/.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-german-s-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-german-s_79-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-german-s_79-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The result of the <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">High German consonant shift</a> produced a different sort of <i>s</i> than the original Proto-Germanic <i>s</i>.  The former was written &#x27e8;z&#x27e9; and the latter &#x27e8;s&#x27e9;.  It is thought that the former was a <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental</a> /s/, somewhat like in English, while the latter was an "<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_apicoalveolar_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless apicoalveolar fricative">apicoalveolar</a>" sound as in modern European Spanish, sounding somewhere between English /s/ and /\xca\x83/. (<cite class="citation journal">Joos, Martin (1952). "The Medieval Sibilants". <i>Language</i>. <b>28</b> (2): 222\xe2\x80\x93231. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.2307%2F410515">10.2307/410515</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Language&amp;rft.atitle=The+Medieval+Sibilants&amp;rft.volume=28&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=222-231&amp;rft.date=1952&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F410515&amp;rft.aulast=Joos&amp;rft.aufirst=Martin&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGermanic+languages" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/>) Modern standard German has /\xca\x83/ for this sound in some contexts, e.g. initially before a consonant (<i>schlimm</i> cf. English <i>slim</i>; <i>Stand</i> /\xca\x83tant/, cf. English <i>stand</i>), and after /r/ (<i>Arsch</i>, cf. English <i>arse</i>). A number of modern southern German dialects have /\xca\x83/ for this sound before all consonants, whether or not word-initially.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-oe-palatal-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-oe-palatal_80-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oe-palatal_80-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oe-palatal_80-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Old English <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalizes</a> /k,g,\xc9\xa3/ to /t\xca\x83,d\xca\x92,j/ near a front vowel.  The sounds /k/ and /\xc9\xa3/ palatalized initially before any front vowel.  Elsewhere /\xc9\xa3/ palatalized before /j/ or <i>before or after</i> any front vowel, where /k/ and /g/ (which occurred only in the combinations /gg/, /ng/) palatalized before /j/, or either before or after /i,i\xcb\x90/.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-voiced-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-voiced_81-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-voiced_81-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-voiced_81-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Voiced fricatives were originally allophones of voiced stops, when occurring after a vowel or after certain consonants (and for /g/, also initially \xe2\x80\x94 hard [g] occurred only in the combinations /gg/, /ng/).  In Old Norse and Old English, voiceless fricatives became voiced between vowels (and finally after a vowel in Old Norse); as a result, voiced fricatives were reanalyzed as allophones of voiceless fricatives.  In Old High German, all voiced fricatives hardened into stops.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-r-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-r_82-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the early periods of the various languages, the sound written /r/ may have been strongly <a href="/wiki/Velarized" class="mw-redirect" title="Velarized">velarized</a>, as in modern <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> (Lass 1994); this is one possible explanation for the various processes were triggered by <i>h</i> (probably <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[x]</span>) and <i>r</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-lost-n-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lost-n_83-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lost-n_83-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Old English and Old Norse lose /n/ before certain consonants, with the previous vowel lengthened (in Old Norse, the following consonant is also lengthened).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">/n/ lost finally and before /s,p,t,k/, but not before other consonants.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-jw-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-jw_85-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-jw_85-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Proto-Germanic /j/ and /w/ were often lost between vowels in all languages, often with /j/ or /w/ later reappearing to break the hiatus, and not always corresponding to the sound previously present.  After a consonant, Gothic consistently preserved /j/ and /w/, but most languages deleted /j/ (after triggering <a href="/wiki/I-umlaut" class="mw-redirect" title="I-umlaut">i-umlaut</a>), and /w/ sometimes disappeared.  The loss of /j/ after a consonant occurred in the various languages at different times and to differing degrees.  For example, /j/ was still present in most circumstances in written Old Saxon, and was still present in Old Norse when a short vowel preceded and a back vowel followed; but in Old English and Old High German, /j/ only remained after an /r/ preceded by a short vowel.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The oldest Germanic languages have the typical complex inflected morphology of old <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European languages</a>, with four or five noun cases; verbs marked for person, number, tense and mood; multiple noun and verb classes; few or no articles; and rather free word order.  The old Germanic languages are famous for having only two tenses (present and past), with three PIE past-tense aspects (imperfect, aorist, and perfect/stative) merged into one and no new tenses (future, pluperfect, etc.) developing.  There were three moods: indicative, subjunctive (developed from the PIE <a href="/wiki/Optative_mood" title="Optative mood">optative mood</a>) and imperative.  Gothic verbs had a number of archaic features inherited from PIE that were lost in the other Germanic languages with few traces, including dual endings, an inflected passive voice (derived from the PIE <a href="/wiki/Mediopassive_voice" title="Mediopassive voice">mediopassive voice</a>), and a class of verbs with reduplication in the past tense (derived from the PIE perfect).  The complex tense system of modern English (e.g. <i>In three months, the house will still be being built</i> or <i>If you had not acted so stupidly, we would never have been caught</i>) is almost entirely due to subsequent developments (although paralleled in many of the other Germanic languages).\n</p><p>Among the primary innovations in Proto-Germanic are the <a href="/wiki/Preterite_present_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Preterite present verb">preterite present verbs</a>, a special set of verbs whose present tense looks like the past tense of other verbs and which is the origin of most <a href="/wiki/Modal_verb" title="Modal verb">modal verbs</a> in English; a past-tense ending (in the so-called "weak verbs", marked with <i>-ed</i> in English) that appears variously as /d/ or /t/, often assumed to be derived from the verb "to do"; and two separate sets of adjective endings, originally corresponding to a distinction between indefinite semantics ("a man", with a combination of PIE adjective and pronoun endings) and definite semantics ("the man", with endings derived from PIE <i>n</i>-stem nouns).\n</p><p>Note that most modern Germanic languages have lost most of the inherited inflectional morphology as a result of the steady attrition of unstressed endings triggered by the strong initial stress. (Contrast, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic languages</a>, which have largely kept the Indo-European <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> and consequently preserved much of the inherited morphology.) <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and to a lesser extent modern German best preserve the Proto\xe2\x80\x93Germanic inflectional system, with four noun cases, three genders, and well-marked verbs. English and Afrikaans are at the other extreme, with almost no remaining inflectional morphology.\n</p><p>The following shows a typical masculine <i>a</i>-stem noun, Proto-Germanic <i>*fiskaz</i> ("fish"), and its development in the various old literary languages:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Declension of <i>a</i>-stem noun <i>*fiskaz</i> "fish" in various languages<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2"></th>\n<th>Proto-Germanic</th>\n<th>Gothic</th>\n<th>Old Norse</th>\n<th>Old High German</th>\n<th>Middle High German</th>\n<th>Modern German</th>\n<th>Old English</th>\n<th>Old Saxon</th>\n<th>Old Frisian\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th width="80" rowspan="6">Singular\n</th>\n<th>Nominative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-az</td>\n<td>fisk-s</td>\n<td rowspan="2">fisk-r</td>\n<td rowspan="3">visk</td>\n<td rowspan="3">visch</td>\n<td rowspan="3">Fisch</td>\n<td rowspan="3">fisc</td>\n<td rowspan="3">fisc</td>\n<td rowspan="3">fisk\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Vocative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk</td>\n<td>fisk\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Accusative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-\xc4\x85</td>\n<td>fisk</td>\n<td>fisk\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Genitive\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-as, -is</td>\n<td>fisk-is</td>\n<td>fisk-s</td>\n<td>visk-es</td>\n<td>visch-es</td>\n<td>Fisch-es<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>fisc-es &lt; fisc-i<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>fisc-u</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th width="80" rowspan="5">Plural\n</th>\n<th>Nominative, Vocative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-\xc3\xb4s, -\xc3\xb4z</td>\n<td>fisk-\xc5\x8ds</td>\n<td>fisk-ar</td>\n<td>visk-a</td>\n<td rowspan="3">visch-e</td>\n<td rowspan="3">Fisch-e</td>\n<td rowspan="2">fisc-as</td>\n<td rowspan="2">fisc-\xc5\x8ds, -\xc4\x81s</td>\n<td rowspan="2">fisk-ar, -a\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Accusative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-anz</td>\n<td>fisk-ans</td>\n<td>fisk-a</td>\n<td>visk-\xc4\x81\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Genitive\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-\xc7\xab\xcc\x82</td>\n<td>fisk-\xc4\x93</td>\n<td>fisk-a</td>\n<td>visk-\xc5\x8d</td>\n<td>fisc-a</td>\n<td>fisc-\xc5\x8d, -\xc4\x81</td>\n<td>fisk-a\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Dative\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-amaz</td>\n<td>fisk-am</td>\n<td>fisk-um, -om</td>\n<td>visk-um</td>\n<td>visch-en</td>\n<td>Fisch-en</td>\n<td>fisc-um</td>\n<td>fisc-un, -on</td>\n<td>fisk-um, -on, -em\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Instrumental\n</th>\n<td>*fisk-amiz</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x94\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_vs._weak_nouns_and_adjectives">Strong vs. weak nouns and adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Strong vs. weak nouns and adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Originally, adjectives in Proto-Indo-European followed the same declensional classes as nouns. The most common class (the <i>o/\xc4\x81</i> class) used a combination of <i>o</i>-stem endings for masculine and neuter genders and <i>\xc4\x81</i>-stems ending for feminine genders, but other common classes (e.g. the <i>i</i> class and <i>u</i> class) used endings from a single vowel-stem declension for all genders, and various other classes existed that were based on other declensions. A quite different set of "pronominal" endings was used for pronouns, <a href="/wiki/Determiner_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Determiner (linguistics)">determiners</a>, and words with related semantics (e.g., "all", "only").\n</p><p>An important innovation in Proto-Germanic was the development of two separate sets of adjective endings, originally corresponding to a distinction between indefinite semantics ("a man") and definite semantics ("the man"). The endings of indefinite adjectives were derived from a combination of pronominal endings with one of the common vowel-stem adjective declensions \xe2\x80\x93 usually the <i>o/\xc4\x81</i> class (often termed the <i>a/\xc5\x8d</i> class in the specific context of the Germanic languages) but sometimes the <i>i</i> or <i>u</i> classes. Definite adjectives, however, had endings based on <i>n</i>-stem nouns.  Originally both types of adjectives could be used by themselves, but already by Proto-Germanic times a pattern evolved whereby definite adjectives had to be accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Determiner_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Determiner (linguistics)">determiner</a> with definite semantics (e.g., a <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Demonstrative pronoun">demonstrative pronoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive pronoun">possessive pronoun</a>, or the like), while indefinite adjectives were used in other circumstances (either accompanied by a word with indefinite semantics such as "a", "one", or "some" or unaccompanied).\n</p><p>In the 19th century, the two types of adjectives \xe2\x80\x93 indefinite and definite \xe2\x80\x93 were respectively termed "strong" and "weak", names which are still commonly used. These names were based on the appearance of the two sets of endings in modern German. In German, the distinctive case endings formerly present on nouns have largely disappeared, with the result that the load of distinguishing one case from another is almost entirely carried by determiners and adjectives. Furthermore, due to regular sound change, the various definite (<i>n</i>-stem) adjective endings coalesced to the point where only two endings (<i>-e</i> and <i>-en</i>) remain in modern German to express the sixteen possible inflectional categories of the language (masculine/feminine/neuter/plural crossed with nominative/accusative/dative/genitive \xe2\x80\x93 modern German merges all genders in the plural). The indefinite (<i>a/\xc5\x8d</i>-stem) adjective endings were less affected by sound change, with six endings remaining (<i>-, -e, -es, -er, -em, -en</i>), cleverly distributed in a way that is capable of expressing the various inflectional categories without too much ambiguity. As a result, the definite endings were thought of as too "weak" to carry inflectional meaning and in need of "strengthening" by the presence of an accompanying determiner, while the indefinite endings were viewed as "strong" enough to indicate the inflectional categories even when standing alone. (This view is enhanced by the fact that modern German largely uses weak-ending adjectives when accompanying an indefinite article, and hence the indefinite/definite distinction no longer clearly applies.) By analogy, the terms "strong" and "weak" were extended to the corresponding noun classes, with <i>a</i>-stem and <i>\xc5\x8d</i>-stem nouns termed "strong" and <i>n</i>-stem nouns termed "weak".\n</p><p>However, in Proto-Germanic \xe2\x80\x93 and still in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, the most conservative Germanic language \xe2\x80\x93 the terms "strong" and "weak" are not clearly appropriate. For one thing, there were a large number of noun declensions. The <i>a</i>-stem, <i>\xc5\x8d</i>-stem, and <i>n</i>-stem declensions were the most common and represented targets into which the other declensions were eventually absorbed, but this process occurred only gradually. Originally the <i>n</i>-stem declension was not a single declension but a set of separate declensions (e.g., <i>-an</i>, <i>-\xc5\x8dn</i>, <i>-\xc4\xabn</i>) with related endings, and these endings were in no way any "weaker" than the endings of any other declensions. (For example, among the eight possible inflectional categories of a noun \xe2\x80\x94 singular/plural crossed with nominative/accusative/dative/genitive \xe2\x80\x94 masculine <i>an</i>-stem nouns in Gothic include seven endings, and feminine <i>\xc5\x8dn</i>-stem nouns include six endings, meaning there is very little ambiguity of "weakness" in these endings and in fact much less than in the German "strong" endings.) Although it is possible to group the various noun declensions into three basic categories \xe2\x80\x94 vowel-stem, <i>n</i>-stem, and other-consonant-stem (a.k.a. "minor declensions") \xe2\x80\x94 the vowel-stem nouns do not display any sort of unity in their endings that supports grouping them together with each other but separate from the <i>n</i>-stem endings.\n</p><p>It is only in later languages that the binary distinction between "strong" and "weak" nouns become more relevant. In <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, the <i>n</i>-stem nouns form a single, clear class, but the masculine <i>a</i>-stem and feminine <i>\xc5\x8d</i>-stem nouns have little in common with each other, and neither has much similarity to the small class of <i>u</i>-stem nouns. Similarly, in <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, the masculine <i>a</i>-stem and feminine <i>\xc5\x8d</i>-stem nouns have little in common with each other, and the continuations of the masculine <i>an</i>-stem and feminine <i>\xc5\x8dn/\xc4\xabn</i>-stem nouns are also quite distinct. It is only in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> and modern German that the various vowel-stem nouns have merged to the point that a binary strong/weak distinction clearly applies.\n</p><p>As a result, newer grammatical descriptions of the Germanic languages often avoid the terms "strong" and "weak" except in conjunction with German itself, preferring instead to use the terms "indefinite" and "definite" for adjectives and to distinguish nouns by their actual stem class.\n</p><p>In English, both two sets of adjective endings were lost entirely in the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> period.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Note that divisions between and among subfamilies of Germanic are rarely precisely defined; most form continuous clines, with adjacent <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">varieties</a> being mutually intelligible and more separated ones not.  Within the Germanic language family is <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="East Germanic">East Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="West Germanic">West Germanic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="North Germanic">North Germanic</a>.  However, East Germanic languages became extinct several centuries ago. In some literature, the West Germanic grouping is also called "<a href="/wiki/South_Germanic" title="South Germanic">South Germanic</a>" or is further divided between West (Dutch, English, Frisian) and South Germanic (Yiddish, German) as opposed to forming a single group.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diachronic">Diachronic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Diachronic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The table below shows the succession of the significant historical stages of each <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> (horizontally) and their approximate groupings in <a href="/wiki/Subfamilies" class="mw-redirect" title="Subfamilies">subfamilies</a> (vertically). Vertical sequence within each group does not imply a measure of greater or lesser similarity.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; font-size: 100%">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td href="/wiki/Burgundian_language_(Germanic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundian language (Germanic)">Burgundian</a>\n</td>\n<td colspan="5">extinct\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Germanic-diachronic-1"><b><a href="#ref_Germanic-diachronic-1">^1</a></b></span>  There are conflicting opinions on the classification of <a href="/wiki/Lombardic_language" title="Lombardic language">Lombardic</a>. It has also been classified as close to <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a>.</li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Germanic-diachronic-2"><b><a href="#ref_Germanic-diachronic-2">^2</a></b></span>  <i>Late</i> <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> refers to the post-<a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> period. Especially for the language situation in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> this event was important.</li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Germanic-diachronic-3"><b><a href="#ref_Germanic-diachronic-3">^3</a></b></span>  From Early Northern Middle English.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> McClure gives <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_(Old_English)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian (Old English)">Northumbrian Old English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>Oxford Companion to the English Language</i> (p. 894) the \'sources\' of <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> are described as "the Old English of the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>" and "the Scandinavian-influenced English of immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midland England</a> in the 12-13c [...]." The historical stages \'Early\xe2\x80\x94Middle\xe2\x80\x94Modern Scots\' are used, for example, in the "Concise Scots Dictionary"<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and "A <a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_the_Older_Scottish_Tongue" title="Dictionary of the Older Scottish Tongue">Dictionary of the Older Scottish Tongue</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Germanic-diachronic-4"><b><a href="#ref_Germanic-diachronic-4">^4</a></b></span>  The speakers of <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a> were assimilated to speak <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots</a> varieties (<a href="/wiki/Insular_Scots" title="Insular Scots">Insular Scots</a>).</li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Germanic-diachronic-5"><b><a href="#ref_Germanic-diachronic-5">^5</a></b></span>  Modern Gutnish (<i>Gutam\xc3\xa5l</i>), the direct descendant of Old Gutnish (<i>Gutniska</i>), has been marginalized by the Gotlandic dialect/accent of Standard Swedish (<i>Gotl\xc3\xa4ndska</i>).</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span title="German language">German</a></div> Dots indicate areas where <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">multilingualism</a> is common.</div></div></div>\n<p>All living Germanic languages belong either to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> or to the <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic</a> branch.\nThe West Germanic group is the larger by far, further subdivided into <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Frisian">Anglo-Frisian</a> on one hand and <a href="/wiki/Continental_West_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental West Germanic">Continental West Germanic</a> on the other. Anglo-Frisian notably includes English and all its <a href="/wiki/English_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="English languages">variants</a>, while Continental West Germanic includes German (<a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">standard register</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_dialects" title="German dialects">dialects</a>), as well as Dutch (<a href="/wiki/Standard_Dutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Dutch">standard register</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_dialects" title="Dutch dialects">dialects</a>).\n</p><p>Modern classification looks like this. For a full classification, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Germanic_languages" title="List of Germanic languages">List of Germanic languages</a>.\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" Scandinavian\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dalecarlian_dialects" title="Dalecarlian dialects">Dalecarlian dialects</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Modern_Gutnish" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern Gutnish">Gutnish</a></li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The earliest evidence of Germanic languages comes from names recorded in the 1st century by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> (especially from his work <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i>), but the earliest Germanic writing occurs in a single instance in the 2nd century BC on the <a href="/wiki/Negau_helmet" title="Negau helmet">Negau helmet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From roughly the 2nd century AD, certain speakers of early Germanic varieties developed the <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">Elder Futhark</a>, an early form of the <a href="/wiki/Runic_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Runic alphabet">runic alphabet</a>. Early runic inscriptions also are largely limited to personal names and difficult to interpret. The <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic language</a> was written in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">Gothic alphabet</a> developed by Bishop <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">Ulfilas</a> for his translation of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> in the 4th century.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> priests and monks who spoke and read <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> in addition to their native Germanic varieties began writing the Germanic languages with slightly modified Latin letters. However, throughout the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, runic alphabets remained in common use in Scandinavia.\n</p><p>In addition to the standard <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, many Germanic languages use a variety of <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">accent marks</a> and extra letters, including the <a href="/wiki/%C3%9F" title="\xc3\x9f">\xc3\x9f</a> (<i>Eszett</i>), <a href="/wiki/IJ_(letter)" class="mw-redirect" title="IJ (letter)">\xc4\xb2</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%98" title="\xc3\x98">\xc3\x98</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86" title="\xc3\x86">\xc3\x86</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%85" title="\xc3\x85">\xc3\x85</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%84" title="\xc3\x84">\xc3\x84</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%9C" title="\xc3\x9c">\xc3\x9c</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%96" title="\xc3\x96">\xc3\x96</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%90" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x90">\xc3\x90</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yogh" title="Yogh">\xc8\x9c</a>, and the Latinized runes <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x9e">\xc3\x9e</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wynn" title="Wynn">\xc7\xb7</a> (with its Latin counterpart <a href="/wiki/W" title="W">W</a>). In print, German used to be prevalently set in <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">blackletter</a> <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typefaces</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Fraktur_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fraktur (typeface)">fraktur</a> or <a href="/wiki/Schwabacher" title="Schwabacher">schwabacher</a>) <a href="/wiki/Antiqua%E2%80%93Fraktur_dispute" title="Antiqua\xe2\x80\x93Fraktur dispute">until</a> the 1940s, when <i><a href="/wiki/Kurrent" title="Kurrent">Kurrent</a></i> and, since the early 20th century, <i><a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCtterlin" title="S\xc3\xbctterlin">S\xc3\xbctterlin</a></i> were used for German handwriting.\n</p><p>Yiddish is written using an adapted <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_comparison">Vocabulary comparison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vocabulary comparison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Several of the terms in the table below have had <a href="/wiki/Semantic_drift" class="mw-redirect" title="Semantic drift">semantic drift</a>. For example, the form <i>Sterben</i> and other terms for <i>die</i> are <a href="/wiki/Cognates" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognates">cognates</a> with the English word <i>starve</i>. There is also at least one example of a common borrowing from a non-Germanic source (<i>ounce</i> and its cognates from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>).\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Estimates of native speakers of the Germanic languages vary from 450 million<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> through 500 million and up to more than 520 million. Much of the uncertainty is caused by the rapid spread of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> and conflicting estimates of its native speakers. Here used is the most probable estimate (currently 515 million) as determined by <i>Statistics</i> section below.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There are various conflicting estimates of L1/native users of English, from 360 million up to 430 million and more. English is a current <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>, which is spreading rapidly, often replacing other languages throughout the world, thus making it difficult to provide one definitive number. It is a rare case of a language with many more secondary speakers than natives.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This phenomenon is not restricted to German, but constitutes <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">a common linguistic development</a> affecting all modern day living major languages with a complex set of dialects. As local dialects increasingly cease to be used, they are usually being replaced by a standardized version of the language.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It uses the lowest estimate for English (360 million).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Estimates for English, German and Dutch are less precise than these for the rest of the Germanic languages. These three languages are the most widely spoken ones; the rest are largely concentrated in specific places (excluding Yiddish and Afrikaans), so precise estimates are easier to get.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Estimate includes most <a href="/wiki/High_German" class="mw-redirect" title="High German">High German</a> dialects classified into the German language spectrum, while leaves some out like the <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish language</a>. <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> is regarded separately.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All other Germanic languages, including <a href="/wiki/Gutnish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutnish language">Gutnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalecarlian_dialects" title="Dalecarlian dialects">Dalecarlian dialects</a> (among them <a href="/wiki/Elfdalian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Elfdalian language">Elfdalian</a>) and any other minor languages.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Estimates of native speakers of the Germanic languages vary from 450 million<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> through 500 million and up to more than 520 million. Much of the uncertainty is caused by the rapid spread of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> and conflicting estimates of its native speakers. Here used is the most probable estimate as determined by <i>Statistics</i> section.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Glottolog-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Glottolog_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Hammarstr\xc3\xb6m, Harald; Forkel, Robert; Haspelmath, Martin, eds.
